   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Solar cycle
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about the sunspot cycle. For the 28-year cycle of the
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
        Julian calendar
       </a>
       , see
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle_(calendar)" title="Solar cycle (calendar)">
        Solar cycle (calendar)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunspot_Numbers.png">
         <img alt="Line graph showing historical sunspot number count, Maunder and Dalton minima, and the Modern Maximum" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="1020" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Sunspot_Numbers.png/400px-Sunspot_Numbers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Sunspot_Numbers.png/600px-Sunspot_Numbers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Sunspot_Numbers.png/800px-Sunspot_Numbers.png 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunspot_Numbers.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         400 year sunspot history, including the
         <a href="/wiki/Maunder_Minimum" title="Maunder Minimum">
          Maunder Minimum
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif/400px-Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif/600px-Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif/800px-Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Cycle_Prediction.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         "The current prediction for Sunspot Cycle 24 gives a smoothed sunspot number maximum of about 69 in the late Summer of 2013. The smoothed sunspot number reached 68.9 in August 2013 so the official maximum will be at least this high. The smoothed sunspot number has been rising again towards this second peak over the last five months and has now surpassed the level of the first peak (66.9 in February 2012). Many cycles are double peaked but this is the first in which the second peak in sunspot number was larger than the first. We are currently over five years into Cycle 24. The current predicted and observed size makes this the smallest sunspot cycle since Cycle 14 which had a maximum of 64.2 in February of 1906."
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-1">
           [1]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        solar cycle
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        solar magnetic activity cycle
       </b>
       is the nearly periodic 11-year change in the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       's activity (including changes in the levels of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">
        solar radiation
       </a>
       and ejection of solar material) and appearance (changes in the number and size of
       <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">
        sunspots
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
        flares
       </a>
       , and other manifestations).
      </p>
      <p>
       They have been observed (by changes in the sun's appearance and by changes seen on Earth, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
        auroras
       </a>
       ) for centuries.
      </p>
      <p>
       The changes on the Sun cause effects in space, in the atmosphere, and on Earth's surface. While it is the dominant variable in solar activity, aperiodic fluctuations also occur.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv/512px--Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="52.500317460317" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 512 × 288 (361 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="512" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="361143" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="173784" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="265232" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:Evolution of Magnetism on the Sun.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv/220px--Evolution_of_Magnetism_on_the_Sun.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>

          <span class="play-btn-large">

           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33">
         <a href="#External_links">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           11
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           External links
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">
        Definition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Solar_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Solar cycles have an average duration of about 11 years.
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">
        Solar maximum
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_minimum" title="Solar minimum">
        solar minimum
       </a>
       refer respectively to periods of maximum and minimum sunspot counts. Cycles span from one minimum to the next.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_history">
        Observational history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Solar_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Observational history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_solar_observation" title="History of solar observation">
        History of solar observation
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:396px;max-width:396px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SHSchwabe.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="283" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/SHSchwabe.jpg/200px-SHSchwabe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/SHSchwabe.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Samuel Heinrich Schwabe (1789–1875). German astronomer, discovered the solar cycle through extended observations of sunspots
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:190px;max-width:190px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RudolfWolf.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="357" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/RudolfWolf.jpg/188px-RudolfWolf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/RudolfWolf.jpg/282px-RudolfWolf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/RudolfWolf.jpg 2x" width="188"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Rudolf Wolf (1816–1893), Swiss astronomer, carried out historical reconstruction of solar activity back to the seventeenth century
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The solar cycle was discovered in 1843 by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Heinrich_Schwabe" title="Samuel Heinrich Schwabe">
        Samuel Heinrich Schwabe
       </a>
       , who after 17 years of observations noticed a periodic variation in the average number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspots" title="Sunspots">
        sunspots
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwabe_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schwabe-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Wolf" title="Rudolf Wolf">
        Rudolf Wolf
       </a>
       compiled and studied these and other observations, reconstructing the cycle back to 1745, eventually pushing these reconstructions to the earliest observations of sunspots by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">
        Galileo
       </a>
       and contemporaries in the early seventeenth century.
      </p>
      <p>
       Following Wolf's numbering scheme, the 1755–1766 cycle is traditionally numbered "1". Wolf created a standard sunspot number index, the Wolf index, which continues to be used today.
      </p>
      <p>
       The period between 1645 and 1715, a time of few sunspots,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maunder_minimum" title="Maunder minimum">
        Maunder minimum
       </a>
       , after
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Walter_Maunder" title="Edward Walter Maunder">
        Edward Walter Maunder
       </a>
       , who extensively researched this peculiar event, first noted by
       <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Sp%C3%B6rer" title="Gustav Spörer">
        Gustav Spörer
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the second half of the nineteenth century
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Christopher_Carrington" title="Richard Christopher Carrington">
        Richard Carrington
       </a>
       and Spörer independently noted the phenomena of sunspots appearing at different latitudes at different parts of the cycle.
      </p>
      <p>
       The cycle's physical basis was elucidated by
       <a href="/wiki/George_Ellery_Hale" title="George Ellery Hale">
        Hale
       </a>
       and collaborators, who in 1908 showed that sunspots were strongly magnetized (the first detection of magnetic fields beyond the Earth). In 1919 they showed that the magnetic polarity of sunspot pairs:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Is constant throughout a cycle;
       </li>
       <li>
        Is opposite across the equator throughout a cycle;
       </li>
       <li>
        Reverses itself from one cycle to the next.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Hale's observations revealed that the complete magnetic cycle spans two solar cycles, or 22 years, before returning to its original state. However, because nearly all manifestations are insensitive to polarity, the "11-year solar cycle" remains the focus of research.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1961 the father-and-son team of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harold_Babcock" title="Harold Babcock">
        Harold
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horace_Babcock" title="Horace Babcock">
        Horace Babcock
       </a>
       established that the solar cycle is a spatiotemporal magnetic process unfolding over the Sun as a whole. They observed that the solar surface is magnetized outside of sunspots; that this (weaker) magnetic field is to first order a
       <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">
        dipole
       </a>
       ; and that this dipole undergoes polarity reversals with the same period as the sunspot cycle. Horace's
       <a href="/wiki/Babcock_Model" title="Babcock Model">
        Babcock model
       </a>
       described the Sun's oscillatory magnetic field, with a quasi-steady periodicity of 22 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oma.be_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-oma.be-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwabe_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schwabe-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It covered the oscillatory exchange of energy between poloidal and toroidal solar magnetic field ingredients. The two halves of the 22-year cycle are not identical, typically alternating cycles show higher (lower) sunspot counts (the
       <a href="/wiki/Gnevyshev-Ohl_rule" title="Gnevyshev-Ohl rule">
        Gnevyshev-Ohl rule
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hathaway_review_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hathaway_review-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       )
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cycle_history">
        Cycle history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Solar_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cycle history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunspots_11000_years.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1360" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sunspots_11000_years.svg/300px-Sunspots_11000_years.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sunspots_11000_years.svg/450px-Sunspots_11000_years.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sunspots_11000_years.svg/600px-Sunspots_11000_years.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunspots_11000_years.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Reconstruction of solar activity over 11,400 years. Period of equally high activity over 8,000 years ago marked.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sunspot numbers over the past 11,400 years have been reconstructed using
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">
        Carbon-14
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Dendroclimatology" title="Dendroclimatology">
        dendroclimatology
       </a>
       . The level of solar activity beginning in the 1940s is exceptional – the last period of similar magnitude occurred around 9,000 years ago (during the warm
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreal_(period)" title="Boreal (period)">
        Boreal period
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Usoskin07_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Usoskin07-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solanski2004_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solanski2004-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Sun was at a similarly high level of magnetic activity for only ~10% of the past 11,400 years. Almost all earlier high-activity periods were shorter than the present episode.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solanski2004_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solanski2004-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fossil records suggest that the Solar cycle has been stable for at least the last 290 million years. For example, the cycle length during the early
       <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">
        Permian
       </a>
       is estimated to be 10.62 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luthardt2017_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Luthardt2017-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="885" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg/350px-Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg/525px-Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg/700px-Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon14_with_activity_labels.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Solar activity events recorded in radiocarbon. Present period is on right. Values since 1900 not shown.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SedimentStudy_10-0">

           [10]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </td>

       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>

         </a>
        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>

         </a>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         1820
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

         <a href="/wiki/Modern_Maximum" title="Modern Maximum">
